[0:0:400,335] Natural  

[1:0:379,366] Resources  

[2:0:344,409] Natural Resources Agency programs protect and  

[2:1:344,418] restore California's diverse natural and cultural  

[2:2:344,428] resources for current and future generations,  

[3:0:344,438] including operating state parks, managing water  

[3:1:344,448] supplies, supporting forest health, encouraging  

[3:2:344,458] clean energy, and preserving fish and wildlife  

[3:3:344,468] habitats, rich farmlands and mineral resources.  

[3:4:344,478] Agency programs also protect the public through  

[3:5:344,488] construction and maintenance of levees in the  

[4:0:344,498] Central Valley and suppression of wildfires. These  

[4:1:344,508] programs not only contribute to the state's unique  

[4:2:344,517] quality of life, but also are critical to sustaining a  

[4:3:344,527] vibrant economy.

[1:0:242,39] 3100   Exposition Park  

[2:0:66,61] The Office of Exposition Park Management, the Science Center, and the California African American Museum (CAAM), are  

[2:1:66,71] located in Exposition Park, a 152-acre tract in south Los Angeles, which is owned by the state. For budget purposes, these  

[2:2:66,81] three departments are collectively known as Exposition Park.  

[3:0:66,99] The Office of Exposition Park Management provides long-term leadership in the development and implementation of park  

[3:1:66,109] usage policy and day-to-day management, operation and promotion of the park for its tenants and the public. The mission of  

[3:2:66,119] the Science Center is to stimulate curiosity and inspire science learning. The mission of CAAM is to research, collect, preserve,  

[3:3:66,129] and interpret, for public enrichment, the history, art, and culture of African Americans with an emphasis on California and the  

[3:4:66,139] western United States.  

[4:0:66,157] Because   the Science Center's   programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the   Science Center   has a capital outlay  

[4:1:66,167] program to support this need. For the specifics on the Science Center's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[5:0:66,355] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,375] 2300 - CALIFORNIA SCIENCE CENTER  

[7:0:66,395] Attracting over 2 million guests annually, the California Science Center represents one of California's premier educational and  

[7:1:66,405] family destinations. Governed by a nine-member Board of Directors appointed by the Governor, the Science Center develops  

[7:2:66,415] and features award-winning exhibits and internationally renowned education programs.  

[8:0:66,436] Hands-on educational exhibits and programs focus on science, math, technology, and conservation which explore biological  

[8:1:66,446] processes of humans, animals, plants, the Earth's ecosystems, habitats, and geophysical processes, as well as engineering,  

[8:2:66,456] communications, and transportation, on land and in space. The Science Center’s Air and Space Center, the permanent home  

[8:3:66,466] for the Space Shuttle Endeavour, will be constructed by the California Science Center Foundation. In July 2021, the Foundation  

[8:4:66,477] reached the fundraising threshold required to begin construction. The entire bond financing process was completed in October  

[8:5:66,487] 2021, and construction is expected to begin in early 2022.  

[9:0:66,507] In addition, the Science Center operates a 3D IMAX theater which features science related films and documentaries. The  

[9:1:66,517] Center for Science Learning offers professional development programs to improve math and science skills of teachers and  

[9:2:66,528] other educators. The Science Center School is a K-5 science, math and technology focused neighborhood charter school. As a  

[9:3:66,538] Title I school, it serves one of the more underserved and economically challenged communities in South Los Angeles. The  

[9:4:66,548] school's instructional programs and teacher training meet California science standards.  

[10:0:66,568] The Science Center's mission values accessibility and inclusiveness and strives to inspire interest in science among those  

[10:1:66,579] traditionally underrepresented in science, math, technology and engineering. The Science Center receives significant funding  

[10:2:66,589] support for exhibit development and capital outlay projects from the California Science Center Foundation.  

[11:0:66,607] 2305 - EXPOSITION PARK MANAGEMENT  

[12:0:66,627] The Office of Exposition Park Management (OEPM) is responsible for public safety operations, parking services management  

[12:1:66,637] and overall park beautification maintenance. In addition, OEPM administers supervision and coordination of park-wide events,  

[12:2:66,647] and facilitates communication efforts between state, city, county, and private entities represented within the park. OEPM is  

[12:3:66,658] responsible for the negotiations of ground leases and agreements which impact the state's interest, including development of  

[12:4:66,668] park facilities and future infrastructure improvements. OEPM is also responsible for managing the Exposition Park  

[12:5:66,678] Improvement Fund and generating revenue to maintain the park. Exposition Park enriches the internal park community and in  

[12:6:66,688] addition provides green space and recreational areas for the surrounding neighborhood.  

[13:0:66,706] 2310 - CALIFORNIA AFRICAN AMERICAN MUSEUM  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:417,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — RES   3 

[2:0:207,32] 3100   Exposition Park - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The California African American Museum (CAAM) is governed by a seven-member board of directors appointed by the  

[3:1:66,79] Governor. CAAM’s mission is to research, collect, preserve, and interpret, for public enrichment, the history, art, and culture of  

[3:2:66,89] African Americans with an emphasis on California and the western United States. Founded in 1977, as the first African  

[3:3:66,99] American museum of art, history, and culture fully supported by a state, CAAM was the direct result of a sustained, multiyear  

[3:4:66,109] campaign of activism undertaken by visionary founders and community members. CAAM occupies a 44,000-square-foot facility  

[3:5:66,120] in Exposition Park, which includes 15,000 square feet of gallery space and an additional 13,000 square feet of space for public  

[3:6:66,130] programming. The museum’s permanent collection houses 4,000 objects that span paintings, photographs, film, sculpture,  

[3:7:66,140] historical documents, and artifacts. CAAM has a strong mandate to support the work of under-represented artists of color and  

[3:8:66,150] to think expansively about the way that people of the African diaspora have contributed to American culture and the world at  

[3:9:66,160] large. That is achieved through a rotating exhibition schedule and public programming for visitors of all ages. More than forty  

[3:10:66,171] years after its founding, the originating principle for CAAM remains vital to a healthy and just America--to create and support an  

[3:11:66,181] inclusive, accessible, and dynamic space where all people can see black lives, culture, and experiences valued and reflected.

[13:0:201,522] 3125   California Tahoe Conservancy  

[14:0:66,543] The California Tahoe Conservancy's mission is to lead California's efforts to restore and enhance the natural and recreational  

[14:1:66,553] resources of the Lake Tahoe Basin (Basin). The Conservancy achieves its mission by developing and implementing programs  

[14:2:66,564] that acquire environmentally sensitive land; provide grants for or directly fund soil erosion prevention and watershed  

[14:3:66,574] restoration; and improve stream environments, wildlife habitats, forest health, and water quality. The Conservancy balances  

[14:4:66,584] these activities with programs to improve public access and recreational opportunities in an environmentally sound manner by  

[14:5:66,594] acquiring lakefront property, constructing pedestrian and bike paths, and building public access facilities.  

[15:0:66,615] The Conservancy is also a key implementing agency of the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP), which is intended to  

[15:1:66,625] restore and protect the Basin's natural resources.   The EIP leverages funding from the states of California and Nevada, the  

[15:2:66,635] Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local governments, and various private entities for projects needed  

[15:3:66,645] to achieve environmental goals in the Basin. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects,  

[15:4:66,655] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and inclusion of new projects. 

[16:0:66,676] Because the Conservancy's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Conservancy has a capital outlay program  

[16:1:66,686] to support this need. For the specifics on the Conservancy's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."  

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:0:66,427] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,447] 2340 - TAHOE CONSERVANCY  

[6:0:66,464] The Conservancy owns and manages nearly 4,700 properties totaling more than 6,500 acres, including thousands of quarter-

[6:1:66,475] acre lots within the Basin’s urban areas. These parcels provide open space, water quality and recreational benefits, and have  

[6:2:66,485] significantly reduced the potential level of development in the Basin. The Conservancy manages its lands for multiple benefits  

[6:3:66,495] and inspects each parcel once every two years to monitor forest health and to identify maintenance needs and encroachments.  

[6:4:66,505] Maintaining healthy forests in urban areas to help protect life and property constitutes another fundamental role of the  

[6:5:66,515] Conservancy’s land management program. The Conservancy also manages 11 properties with recreational amenities,  

[6:6:66,526] including 6 beaches that provide public access to Lake Tahoe.  

[7:0:66,543] The Conservancy and its partners are increasingly shifting to large landscape, multi-jurisdictional, multiple-benefit projects to  

[7:1:66,554] more rapidly and comprehensively achieve restoration goals in the Basin. The Conservancy plans to continue working with a  

[7:2:66,564] wide variety of partners to restore the health and resilience of the Basin’s forests and watersheds, with the aim of also  

[7:3:66,574] increasing the pace and scale of restoration. This includes improving forest diversity, protecting wildlife, applying beneficial fire,  

[7:4:66,584] clearing meadows of encroaching trees, reducing streambank erosion, reconnecting floodplains, and treating storm water. This  

[7:5:66,594] work will enhance the ability of these lands to respond to wildfire, drought, insects, and climate change.  

[7:6:66,612] The Conservancy also works closely with local governments to meet its mission by providing grants for acquisitions and site  

[7:7:66,622] improvements such as building roadside and storm drainage facilities and sediment basins, building bike trails, and creating  

[7:8:66,632] public gathering spaces and improving public access to the Lake.  

[7:9:66,650] 2345 - ACQUISITION AND SITE IMPROVEMENT PROJECTS  

[8:0:66,671] This program acquires environmentally sensitive lands and provides public access and recreation by enhancing existing  

[8:1:66,681] facilities and establishing new public access sites along the Lake and in natural areas. The program also preserves a wide  

[8:2:66,691] variety of habitats supporting endangered, threatened or rare animal and plant species, and restores streams and watersheds  

[8:3:66,701] such as marshes, meadows and riparian areas to mitigate development damage and enhance water quality.  

[9:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] In addition, the program is helping to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and achieve environmental benefits by promoting  

[3:1:66,79] urban in-fill development in the Basin. These efforts include selling, leasing, or exchanging non-environmentally sensitive  

[3:2:66,89] Conservancy lands and associated development rights to support revitalization efforts consistent with local area plans and town  

[3:3:66,99] center objectives.   Property exchanges and the proceeds from the sale or leasing of property are then used to (1) acquire other  

[3:4:66,109] environmentally sensitive lands or (2) fund conservation and other projects that support the Conservancy in fulfilling its mission.

[4:0:66,142] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[5:0:66,161] The California Tahoe Conservancy manages programs to help protect Lake Tahoe's water quality and conserve wildlife habitat,  

[5:1:66,171] watershed areas, and public access on the California side of the Lake Tahoe Basin. The Conservancy is a primary implementer  

[5:2:66,181] of the State of California's responsibilities under the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP) for the Lake Tahoe Basin - a  

[5:3:66,192] 1997 agreement between California, Nevada, the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local  

[5:4:66,202] governments, and various private entities. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects,  

[5:5:66,212] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and new projects.

[10:0:188,405] 3210   Environmental Protection Program  

[11:0:66,426] The California Environmental Protection Program receives revenue from the sale of personalized motor vehicle license plates  

[11:1:66,437] by the Department of Motor Vehicles. Revenues are deposited in the California Environmental License Plate Fund. Funds  

[11:2:66,447] expended pursuant to Division 13.5 of the Public Resources Code must have one or more of the following purposes:  

[11:3:65,465] • The control and abatement of air pollution, including all phases of research into the sources, dynamics and effects of  

[11:4:76,475] environmental pollutants.  

[12:0:65,491] • The acquisition, preservation, restoration, or any combination thereof, of natural areas or ecological reserves.  

[12:1:65,507] • Environmental education, including formal school programs and informal public education programs.  

[12:2:65,523] • Protection of nongame species and threatened and endangered plants and animals.  

[12:3:65,540] • Protection, enhancement, and restoration of fish and wildlife habitat and related water quality, including review of the  

[12:4:76,550] potential impact of development activities and land use changes on that habitat.  

[12:5:65,566] • The purchase, on an opportunity basis, of real property consisting of sensitive natural areas for the state park system and for  

[12:6:76,576] local and regional parks.  

[13:0:65,592] • Reduction or minimization of the effects of soil erosion and the discharge of sediment into the waters of the Lake Tahoe  

[13:1:76,603] region, including the restoration of disturbed wetlands and stream environment zones.  

[14:0:66,630] The appropriations and expenditures for programs and projects funded from the California Environmental License Plate Fund  

[14:1:66,641] are contained in the budgets of the various state boards, commissions and departments.  

[15:0:66,668] LEGAL CITATIONS AND AUTHORITY  

[15:1:66,687] DEPARTMENT AUTHORITY  

[16:0:66,707] Public Resources Code, Division 13.5.

[6:0:200,511] 3340   California Conservation Corps  

[7:0:66,532] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) provides young women and men the opportunity to work hard responding to fires,  

[8:0:66,542] floods and other disasters, restoring California's environment, and installing clean energy and energy conservation measures at  

[8:1:66,552] public facilities throughout the state. Through their service, the corpsmembers gain life, work, and academic skills to become  

[8:2:66,563] strong workers and citizens.  

[9:0:66,583] In addition to the CCC, there are also 14 local conservation corps located in metropolitan communities throughout the state that  

[9:1:66,593] are annually certified by the CCC, and engage young people in conservation, recycling, education, and training activities.  

[10:0:66,614] Because the   CCC's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the CCC has a capital outlay program to support this  

[10:1:66,624] need. For the specifics on the CCC's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[7:0:66,406] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[8:0:74,426] ◦ Wildfire and Forest Resilience Package—The Budget includes $400 million General Fund in 2022-23 as part of a $1.2  

[8:1:85,436] billion package of investments that target the state’s wildfire risks. Of the total $1.2 billion, $730 million will be allocated at  

[8:2:85,446] a later time.   This includes $15 million in 2022-23 for the California Conservation Corps to invest in the Forestry Corps.  

[8:3:74,462] ◦ Enhancing and Expanding California Conservation Corps Fire Crews—The Budget includes $8.1 million General Fund,  

[8:4:85,473] with an offsetting decrease of $1.8 million Collins-Dugan Reimbursement Account, to support additional CAL FIRE/Corps  

[8:5:85,483] fire crews to enhance CAL FIRE’S fire prevention and suppression capabilities.  

[9:0:74,499] ◦ Equipment Replacement—The Budget includes $7.3 million one-time General Fund to replace a portion of an aging fleet,  

[9:1:85,509] which are critical in transporting crews to project sites and emergency response locations.  

[10:0:74,525] ◦ Fire Crews—The Budget includes $1,769,000 General Fund in 2022-23, $1,708,000 in 2023-24, $1,994,000 ongoing, and  

[10:1:85,536] 11 positions, to: (1) right-size existing CCC crews, (2) convert 6 existing seasonal CCC crews to year-round crews, and (3)  

[10:2:85,546] add 4 new year-round crews. These proposals will increase the overall number of permanent fire crews available to: (1)  

[10:3:85,556] respond to wildfires throughout the year, (2) implement high-priority fuel reduction projects to protect communities from  

[10:4:85,566] wildfire, and (3) participate in prescribed fire and other fuels treatment projects to achieve more resilient landscapes. This  

[10:5:85,576] package builds upon existing partnerships and will partially offset the projected loss of Department of Corrections and  

[10:6:85,587] Rehabilitation inmate crews.

[5:0:66,502] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,521] 2360 - TRAINING AND WORK PROGRAM  

[5:2:66,539] This Program focuses on four areas:  

[5:3:66,557] Natural Resource Work: Corpsmembers protect and enhance the state's natural resources through park development, trail  

[5:4:66,567] construction, tree planting, fire hazard reduction, watershed improvement, wildlife habitat enhancement, removal of non-native  

[5:5:66,577] vegetation, meadow restoration, energy and water auditing and retrofitting, irrigation system installation, and drought-tolerant  

[5:6:66,587] and other landscaping.  

[6:0:66,605] Disaster Response: Corpsmembers are dispatched to fires, assisting with initial attack, mop-up and logistical support; floods,  

[6:1:66,615] filling sandbags, reinforcing levees and stabilizing hillsides; earthquakes, removing hazards and staffing disaster assistance  

[6:2:66,625] centers; oil spill clean-up; snow removal; search and rescue operations; pest infestation eradication; and homeland security  

[6:3:66,636] assistance.  

[7:0:66,653] Corpsmember Education: Corpsmembers are provided opportunities to advance their academic skills while in the CCC through  

[7:1:66,663] local adult and charter schools and community colleges. 

[7:2:66,681] Corpsmember Development and Training: The CCC stresses the development of both a work and service ethic, which includes  

[7:3:66,691] teamwork, self-discipline, leadership, and giving back to California. Corpsmembers learn conservation principles and career  

[7:4:66,702] planning. The CCC also offers training in trail building, first aid, Hazardous Waste Operations and Emergency Response, and  

[7:5:66,712] firefighting certification, which can lead to internship opportunities with various employers in California. 

[8:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:165,32] 3340   California Conservation Corps - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) operates 26 facilities statewide, including 9 residential and 17 non-residential  

[3:1:66,79] facilities in both urban and rural areas. These facilities enable the CCC to achieve its mission of providing employment, training,  

[3:2:66,89] and educational opportunities to young adults. The facilities also allow the CCC to assist federal, state and local agencies, as  

[3:3:66,99] well as nonprofit entities, with the conservation of California's natural resources.  

[3:4:66,117] The CCC residential facilities house 80 to 100 corpsmembers each and operate 24 hours a day, bringing young people  

[3:5:66,127] together from across the state, and increasing capacity for emergency response and conservation projects. Non-residential  

[3:6:66,137] centers range in size from 30 to 60 corpsmembers and are located in both urban and rural communities throughout California.

[2:0:145,32] 3355   Office of Energy Infrastructure Safety - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[3:1:66,88] 2370 - REGULATION OF ENERGY INFRASTRUCTURE SAFETY  

[3:2:66,106] Established pursuant to Chapter 81, Statutes of 2019 (AB 111), the Office is the successor to the Wildfire Safety Division of the  

[3:3:66,116] California Public Utilities Commission as of July 1, 2021 and is authorized to:  

[3:4:65,134] • Oversee and enforce electrical corporations’ compliance with wildfire safety pursuant to Chapter 6 (commencing with Section  

[3:5:76,144] 8385) of Division 4.1.  

[4:0:65,160] • In consultation with the California Wildfire Safety Advisory Board, develop and recommend to the commission performance  

[4:1:76,170] metrics to achieve maximum feasible risk reduction to be used to develop the wildfire mitigation plan and evaluate an  

[4:2:76,181] electrical corporation’s compliance with that plan.  

[4:3:65,197] • Develop a field audit program for wildfire mitigation plan compliance by each electrical corporation.  

[4:4:65,213] • Consult with the Office of Emergency Services in the office’s management and response to utility public safety power shutoff  

[4:5:76,223] events and utility actions for compliance with public safety power shutoff program rules and regulations.  

[4:6:65,239] • Support efforts to assess and analyze fire weather data and other atmospheric conditions that could lead to catastrophic  

[4:7:76,250] wildfires and to reduce the likelihood and severity of wildfire incidents that could endanger the safety of persons, properties,  

[4:8:76,260] and the environment within the state.  

[4:9:65,276] • Retain appropriate staff that includes experts in wildfire, weather, climate change, emergency response, and other relevant  

[4:10:76,286] subject matters.  

[5:0:65,302] • Review, as necessary, in coordination with the California Wildfire Safety Advisory Board and necessary commission staff,  

[5:1:76,313] safety requirements for electrical transmission and distribution infrastructure and infrastructure and equipment attached to  

[5:2:76,323] that electrical infrastructure, and provide recommendations to the commission to address the dynamic risk of climate change  

[5:3:76,333] and to mitigate wildfire risk.  

[5:4:65,349] • Perform regulatory oversight of and support to the California Underground Facilities Safe Excavation Board.

[12:0:105,591] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission  

[13:0:66,612] The Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission (Energy Commission) is responsible for ensuring a  

[13:1:66,622] reliable supply of energy to meet state needs while protecting public health, safety, and the environment. Activities include the  

[13:2:66,633] following: making public policy recommendations regarding energy; collecting targeted energy data and ensuring data is  

[13:3:66,643] managed responsibly; developing and implementing research, development, demonstration, and deployment policies and  

[13:4:66,653] programs that promote strategic energy investments; ensuring energy facilities approved by the Energy Commission are  

[13:5:66,663] designed, constructed, operated, amended, and decommissioned in a manner that protects the environment and public health  

[13:6:66,673] and safety, and in compliance with all applicable laws, ordinances, regulations, and standards; adopting building efficiency  

[13:7:66,684] standards, and adopting and enforcing appliance efficiency standards; promoting development, deployment, and integration of  

[13:8:66,694] zero-emission and near zero-emission transportation infrastructure and technology; and supporting climate change goals  

[13:9:66,704] through integrated resource planning, renewable energy development, and system integration.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[7:1:65,235] • The Budget includes $750 million to support the reliability of the state’s electric grid by developing a program to provide  

[7:2:76,245] financial incentives to reduce electricity demand during extreme events; deploying new backup generation using zero or low

[5:0:66,239] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,259] 2380 - REGULATORY and PLANNING PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,287] The Regulatory and Planning program facilitates markets in providing adequate statewide energy supplies. The program  

[7:1:66,297] certifies power plant sites, develops and implements landscape-scale plans to identify suitable areas for renewable energy  

[7:2:66,307] resources and infrastructure development, designates transmission line corridors, and monitors new energy facilities and fuel  

[7:3:66,317] infrastructure for compliance and supply and distribution adequacy, consistent with state energy policies. The program  

[7:4:66,327] develops state energy policy based on the economic, security, safety, and environmental land-use and climate implications of  

[7:5:66,338] energy supply and demand. The Energy Assessments program develops long-range projections for future energy supplies and  

[7:6:66,348] demand, maintains current information on statewide electrical generation sources, consumption and peak demand, natural gas,  

[7:7:66,358] transportation fuels, and distributed generation. The program develops state energy policy based on the economic, financial,  

[7:8:66,368] security, safety, and environmental implications of supply, demand, and price/cost assessments, and monitors energy markets  

[7:9:66,378] for competitive prices and potential market abuses.  

[8:0:66,396] 2380010 - Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation Program 

[9:0:66,414] The Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation programs are responsible for the following:  

[9:1:66,424] reviewing thermal power plant siting applications 50 megawatts and larger; ensuring power plant owners, operators, and  

[9:2:66,434] developers comply with their specific conditions of certification and all applicable laws and regulations when constructing,  

[9:3:66,444] operating, and closing power plants, and investigating potential violations; reviewing project change petitions to amend filed  

[9:4:66,455] applications; reviewing applications for transmission line corridor designation; developing environmental and land use  

[9:5:66,465] information and evaluating constraints and opportunities for renewable energy infrastructure development; and analyzing  

[9:6:66,475] safety, reliability, efficiency, resilience, and environmental performance issues related to power plants and the state electricity  

[9:7:66,485] transmission grid.  

[10:0:66,503] 2380019 - Electricity Analysis  

[11:0:66,521] The Electricity Analysis program is responsible for evaluating current and future electricity supply market trends and  

[11:1:66,531] infrastructure needs; estimating costs and emissions of various generation types; assessing the adequacy of electricity  

[11:2:66,541] supplies; and analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to electricity. Additionally, this program addresses the  

[11:3:66,551] distribution system and its relationship to distributed generation, including planning and interconnection barriers and provides  

[11:4:66,561] information and recommendations to state agencies, electricity market participants, and the public. The natural gas program  

[11:5:66,572] component is responsible for evaluating current and future natural gas market trends and infrastructure needs, assessing the  

[11:6:66,582] adequacy of natural gas supplies, monitoring the interaction of natural gas and electricity to maintain system reliability,  

[11:7:66,592] analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to natural gas, and leading interagency work groups to monitor short-

[11:8:66,602] term natural gas market trends. The program also collects, monitors, and analyzes data on transportation fuel supply and  

[11:9:66,612] production, fuel infrastructure issues, and responds to energy and fuel shortages and emergencies.  

[12:0:66,630] 2385 - ENERGY RESOURCES CONSERVATION PROGRAM  

[13:0:66,658] The Energy Resources Conservation program objectives include reducing overall energy use and decreasing peak electricity  

[13:1:66,668] demand by identifying energy efficiency opportunities and developing and implementing programs and standards for efficiently  

[13:2:66,678] using energy in all sectors.  

[14:0:66,696] 2385010 - Building and Appliances Program  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 37 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:412,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — RES   35 

[2:0:70,32] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The Building and Appliances program objectives include reducing consumer and business energy use, water use, and peak  

[3:1:66,79] demand by developing and implementing strategies and minimum efficiency and flexible demand standards for appliances,  

[3:2:66,89] equipment, and new and existing residential and nonresidential buildings. The Building and Appliances program provides  

[3:3:66,99] technical assistance on building standards and enforcement support to the building industry and building departments. Further,  

[3:4:66,109] the program maintains a database of efficient appliances and equipment and enforces compliance with the appliance efficiency  

[3:5:66,120] standards.  

[4:0:66,137] 2385019 - Energy Projects Evaluation and Assistance Program  

[5:0:66,155] This program is responsible for providing technical assistance, grants, and loans to improve energy efficiency and reduce  

[5:1:66,165] operating costs of schools, colleges, local jurisdictions, hospitals, and public care facilities.  

[6:0:66,183] 2385028 - Demand Analysis  

[7:0:66,201] The Demand Analysis program is responsible for collecting and analyzing electricity and natural gas consumption data used to  

[7:1:66,211] prepare energy demand forecasts, forecasting peak and total energy consumption by sector, and estimating the amount of  

[7:2:66,221] energy conserved by existing and proposed program activities. The program conducts detailed electricity and natural gas end-

[7:3:66,231] use surveys which serve as a primary data source for energy demand forecasts, conservation potential studies, and market  

[7:4:66,241] and policy impact assessments. The program also performs transportation energy demand forecasting, conducts consumer  

[7:5:66,252] vehicle choice surveys, and assesses the impacts of transportation electrification on California electricity use.  

[8:0:66,269] 2390 - DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,297] The Development Program focuses on transportation and alternatives to conventional fossil fuels. The mission is to support  

[9:1:66,307] provision of adequate and reliable transportation energy to the California transportation sector while balancing economic, public  

[9:2:66,318] health, safety, and environmental consequences. The program also conducts research, development, demonstration,  

[9:3:66,328] commercialization, and deployment activities on energy efficiency, renewable and advanced energy technologies, and  

[9:4:66,338] alternative and renewable fuel and advanced vehicle technologies so that future energy supplies are: cost effective, secure,  

[9:5:66,348] and reliable; enhance environmental quality; and promote state and local economic development. The program provides  

[9:6:66,358] technical assistance, financial assistance, direct technology research and demonstration, technology forecasting, technology  

[9:7:66,369] analysis and evaluation, and knowledge sharing.  

[10:0:66,386] 2390010 - Transportation Technology and Fuels  

[11:0:66,404] The Clean Transportation Program provides financial incentives to develop and deploy innovative technologies, infrastructure,  

[11:1:66,414] and strategies that transform California's fuel and vehicle types to help attain the state's climate change and public health  

[11:2:66,424] policies. The program goal is the development, deployment and integration of zero and near-zero emission fuels and advanced  

[11:3:66,435] vehicle technologies and infrastructure. The program provides analyses and recommendations to guide state energy policy and  

[11:4:66,445] legislation to achieve climate change and air quality objectives, reduce petroleum consumption, and promote economic  

[11:5:66,455] development. The program also provides incentives to workforce development, training, and manufacturing that supports the  

[11:6:66,465] program goals.  

[12:0:66,483] 2390019 - Research and Development  

[13:0:66,501] The Research and Development program spurs innovation in energy efficiency, energy generation, storage, infrastructure  

[13:1:66,511] resiliency, renewable integration, and transportation to meet the state’s clean energy and climate goals. The programs bring  

[13:2:66,521] science and technology breakthroughs from the lab to the market to benefit all Californians, with a focus on ensuring the  

[13:3:66,531] benefits also accrue to under-resourced communities. The programs catalyze a world-class innovation ecosystem that provides  

[13:4:66,541] the resources, expertise, and information to foster and support successful clean energy entrepreneurship across the state. The  

[13:5:66,552] programs equip communities, businesses, and public agencies with breakthrough technology solutions and tools to build a  

[13:6:66,562] safe, resilient, affordable and clean energy system. The programs are administered through strategic performance-based  

[13:7:66,572] competitive solicitations.  

[14:0:66,590] 2390028 - Renewable Energy  

[15:0:66,607] The Renewable Energy program fosters growth of the renewable energy market by providing financial incentives to support the  

[15:1:66,618] development and use of eligible renewable energy products; requiring utilities to disclose information to consumers on their  

[15:2:66,628] power fuel mix; implementing the Renewables Portfolio Standard; and establishing solar equipment rating standards and  

[15:3:66,638] maintaining solar equipment lists meeting the performance and safety standards.  

[16:0:66,656] 2395 - LOAN REPAYMENT PROGRAM  

[17:0:66,684] The Loan Repayment program consists of Conservation and Development program loan repayments deposited into the  

[17:1:66,694] following accounts: State Energy Conservation Assistance Account; Energy Technologies Research, Development and  

[17:2:66,704] Demonstration Account; and Local Government Geothermal Resources Revolving Subaccount.  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:0:188,212] 3460   Colorado River Board of California  

[5:1:66,234] The Colorado River Board protects California's rights and interests in the water and power resources of the Colorado River  

[5:2:66,244] system. The Board works with: other Colorado River Basin states (Arizona,   Colorado, Nevada, New Mexico, Utah, and  

[5:3:66,254] Wyoming), federal agencies, other state agencies, six local agencies (Palo Verde Irrigation District, Imperial Irrigation District,  

[5:4:66,264] Coachella Valley Water District, Metropolitan Water District of Southern California, San Diego County Water Authority, Los  

[5:5:66,274] Angeles Department of Water and Power), Congress, the courts, and Mexico. Its activities include analyses of engineering,  

[5:6:66,285] legal and economic matters concerning the Colorado River resources of the seven basin states and the 1944 United States-

[5:7:66,295] Mexico Water Treaty obligation to deliver Colorado River water to Mexico.

[15:3:76,460] investments that address the state’s climate risks. This includes funding to support a Biomass to Hydrogen pilot program.

[15:5:76,489] Sustainable Agricultural Lands Conservation program.

[16:1:76,515] and 17 positions in 2022-23 and ongoing to continue strengthening enforcement of existing laws and regulations and limit  

[16:2:76,526] the state’s financial liability.  

[17:0:65,542] • Statewide Seismic Hazards Reduction — The Budget includes $25.6 million and 21 permament positions to mitigate the risk  

[17:1:76,554] of loss of life and and catastrophic economic impacts of future urban earthquakes in California.

[5:0:66,614] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,633] 2420 - GEOLOGIC HAZARDS AND MINERAL RESOURCES CONSERVATION  

[5:2:66,651] This program evaluates, assesses and maps the state's geologic and seismologic hazards, such as earthquakes, landslides,  

[5:3:66,661] tsunami and volcanic eruption threats, and hazardous minerals exposures, in order to protect the public health and safety and  

[5:4:66,671] the natural environment; analyzes the state's mineral assets; and maps its mineral resources. Information is used by federal,  

[5:5:66,682] state, and local government agencies, industries and individual businesses, and the public to make informed decisions about  

[5:6:66,692] land use, seismic safety, and mineral development.  

[5:7:66,709] 2425 - GEOLOGIC ENERGY MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[6:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 60 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — RES   58 

[1:0:454,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:171,32] 3480   Department of Conservation - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] This program regulates the drilling, operation, and abandonment of oil, natural gas, and geothermal wells to prevent, as   much  

[3:1:66,79] as possible, damage to life, health, property, and natural resources. The program seeks to protect public health and safety and  

[3:2:66,89] environmental quality, including reduction and mitigation of greenhouse gas emissions associated with the development of  

[3:3:66,99] hydrocarbon and geothermal resources in a manner that meets the energy needs of the state. The state is fully reimbursed for  

[3:4:66,109] program expenditures by annual assessments and fees on the respective industries. 

[3:5:66,127] 2430 - LAND RESOURCE PROTECTION  

[4:0:66,145] This program protects agricultural farmland and open space through various financial incentives. The Williamson Act program  

[4:1:66,155] provides advice on and reviews documents related to changes to Williamson Act contracts between landowners and local  

[4:2:66,165] governments. The California Farmland Conservancy Program provides grants to local governments and nonprofit land trusts  

[4:3:66,175] for planning purposes and for the acquisition of agricultural conservation easements that permanently remove development  

[4:4:66,186] rights, and therefore development pressure, from agricultural lands. The Farmland Mapping and Monitoring Program develops  

[4:5:66,196] maps, statistics, and reports relating to farmland conversion, farmland inventory, and land protection to assist in local land use  

[4:6:66,206] decisions.  

[5:0:66,224] 2435 - MINE RECLAMATION  

[6:0:66,241] This program regulates surface mining operations and monitors local lead agencies to ensure compliance with the Surface  

[6:1:66,252] Mining and Reclamation Act of 1975. It assists cities, counties, state agencies, and mine operators in their efforts to reclaim  

[6:2:66,262] mined lands to beneficial uses. This program also compiles an inventory of the state's estimated 47,000 abandoned mines and  

[6:3:66,272] remediates abandoned mine hazards to protect public safety.  

[6:4:66,290] 2440 - STATE MINING AND GEOLOGY BOARD  

[7:0:66,307] The Board serves as a regulatory and policy body for the state's geology, geologic and seismologic hazards, conservation of  

[7:1:66,318] mineral resources, and reclamation of mined lands. The department's California Geological Survey and the Division of Mine  

[7:2:66,328] Reclamation provide the Board with relevant engineering, technical expertise, and support functions for certain reports, plans,  

[7:3:66,338] and maps. The Board also serves as an appeals body for mining operations that have been issued notice of violation orders to  

[7:4:66,348] comply, or administrative penalties and in cases where the Division of Mine Reclamation contests the adequacy of a local  

[7:5:66,358] government’s approval of a mine operation’s financial assurance cost estimate.

[0:0:66,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — RES   72   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:164,89] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection  

[2:0:66,111] The Department of Forestry and Fire Protection (CAL FIRE) provides all hazard—fire, medical, rescue, and  

[3:0:66,121] disaster—emergency response to the public and provides leadership in the protection of life, property, and natural resources in  

[3:1:66,131] California.  

[4:0:66,152] CAL FIRE safeguards the public through: engineering; research, development and adoption of regulations; fire and life safety  

[4:1:66,162] programs; fire prevention, law enforcement, and public information and education; resource protection; and emergency  

[4:2:66,172] response.  

[5:0:66,192] CAL FIRE limits the damage caused by fires, disasters, environmental degradation, and related emergencies by employing  

[5:1:66,203] diverse yet complementary efforts, including: training California's fire service professionals, public education and prevention  

[5:2:66,213] awareness, responsible stewardship of our natural resources, and natural resource and emergency management.  

[6:0:66,233] CAL FIRE cultivates mutually beneficial and cost-effective relationships with all levels of government and the private sector to  

[6:1:66,243] enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of public safety programs and mitigate emergencies in the urban, rural, and wildland  

[6:2:66,254] areas of California.  

[7:0:66,274] Because CAL FIRE's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, CAL FIRE has a capital outlay program to support  

[7:1:66,284] this need. For the specifics on the CAL FIRE's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[10:0:66,623] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[11:0:66,642] Firefighter Fire Crews —The Budget includes $169 million General Fund in 2022-23, $164.3 million ongoing, and 827  

[11:1:66,652] positions, phased in over four years, to staff and support 17 new Firefighter 1 crews and fund associated equipment and  

[11:2:66,662] special repairs. This increase further supports firefighting capabilities and partially offsets the projected loss of Department of  

[11:3:66,673] Corrections and Rehabilitation inmate crews.  

[12:0:66,690] Wildfire and Forest Resilience Package —The Budget includes $50 million General Fund in 2021-22, $305 million ($200  

[12:1:66,701] million Greenhouse Gas Reduction Fund and $105 million General Fund) in 2022-23, as part of a $1.2 billion package of

[0:0:66,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — RES   74 

[1:0:454,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:129,32] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] investments that target the state’s wildfire risks. This includes funding for various program to support and implement projects  

[3:1:66,79] related to wildfire and forest resilience. Of the $1.2 billion, $530 million will be allocated at a later time.  

[4:0:66,97] Additional Fire Hawk Helicopters —The Budget includes $100 million General Fund in 2022-23, $12.7 million ongoing, and 5  

[4:1:66,107] positions to purchase and operate four additional helicopters to support 24/7 operations of the enhanced helicopter fleet.  

[4:2:66,117] California continues to invest in the acquisition and delivery of twelve S70i Fire Hawk helicopters, with 8 of 12 currently in  

[4:3:66,127] California. These larger helicopters have the capability to drop up to 1,000 gallons of water at a time and operate at night,  

[4:4:66,137] which can significantly slow advancing wildfires. The acquisition of four additional helicopters will provide additional aircraft  

[4:5:66,148] availability to help maintain 24/7 flight operations during critical fire weather conditions when frontline helicopters are due for  

[4:6:66,158] maintenance.  

[5:0:66,175] Firefighter Relief Staffing —The Budget includes $97.2 million General Fund in 2022-23, $84.9 million ongoing, and 455  

[5:1:66,186] positions, phased in over four years, to improve the health and wellness of CAL FIRE firefighters and appropriately staff CAL  

[5:2:66,196] FIRE to meet the demands of wildland firefighting in a changing climate. Over the past decade, firefighters from CAL FIRE  

[5:3:66,206] have battled and endured 14 of the 20 largest wildfires in the state's history. Current conditions appear to indicate above  

[5:4:66,216] average wildfire severity and intensity will continue in the coming years.  

[6:0:66,234] July through December 2022 Fire Protection Augmentation —The Budget includes $83.1 million General Fund one-time to  

[6:1:66,244] augment CAL FIRE’s fire protection resources through December 2022 given current drought conditions, increasing fire  

[6:2:66,254] severity and size, and declining inmate camp populations.  

[7:0:66,272] Exclusive Use Type 1 Helitankers —The budget includes $52 million General Fund and 29 positions annually for three years  

[7:1:66,282] to contract for 10 exclusive use helitankers each year while the state awaits the delivery of retardant dropping C-130 aircrafts  

[7:2:66,292] from the federal government. CAL FIRE operates 12 state-owned firefighting helicopters, and 1 firefighting helicopter under  

[7:3:66,303] contract with San Diego County, located strategically at 11 helitack bases across California. This proposal will augment the  

[7:4:66,313] statewide aviation fleet with ten additional heavy helicopters, each capable of dropping 1,000-2,000 gallons of water at a time.  

[8:0:66,331] Direct Mission Support —The Budget includes $37.8 million ($29.3 million General Fund) in 2022-23, $35.6 million ($27.5  

[8:1:66,341] million General Fund) ongoing, and 190 positions to improve operational effectiveness by strengthening and enhancing the  

[8:2:66,351] core administrative and program support critical to sustain CAL FIRE’s direct mission functions in an increasingly complex  

[8:3:66,361] environment.  

[9:0:66,379] Surge Equipment: Fire Engines and Bulldozers —The Budget includes $35.8 million General Fund in 2022-23 and $2.8  

[9:1:66,389] million ongoing for surge capacity dozers and fire engines. CAL FIRE operates 356 frontline fire engines and 59 bulldozers  

[9:2:66,399] across the state. This equipment requires regular maintenance and can have unexpected mechanical breakdowns. This  

[9:3:66,409] proposal will add two surge engines per unit and contract county, and ten additional bulldozers, to provide additional resources  

[9:4:66,420] that can be staffed during critical fire conditions and resource drawdown.  

[10:0:66,437] Fire Integrated Real-Time Intelligence System —The Budget includes $30 million ongoing General Fund, $5.6 million and 20  

[10:1:66,448] positions in CAL FIRE's budget, to establish the Fire Integrated Real-Time Intelligence System (FIRIS) program to increase the  

[10:2:66,458] real-time information and situational awareness available to the state and California’s mutual aid system responders and  

[10:3:66,468] managers on all hazards events, including wildfires. FIRIS will utilize a combination of aircraft, high-definition real time video,  

[10:4:66,478] and data analysis dispatched 24/7 throughout the state to provide nearly instantaneous, accurate visual situation awareness  

[10:5:66,488] that informs public safety and emergency management operational and tactical decisions. Early detection and rapid intelligence  

[10:6:66,499] allows for the strategic and efficient allocation of resources before, during, and after disaster incidents, and a more effective  

[10:7:66,509] response to reduce loss of life and property. The intelligence gathered will also provide data and support to the Wildfire  

[10:8:66,519] Forecast and Threat Intelligence Integration Center, as established through SB 209 (Chapter 405, Statutes of 2019).

[5:0:66,377] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,396] 2461 - STATE FIRE MARSHAL  

[7:0:66,414] The Office of the State Fire Marshal protects life and property through the development and application of fire prevention,  

[7:1:66,424] engineering, education, enforcement, and regulations.  

[8:0:66,442] 2461010 - Office of the State Fire Marshal:  

[9:0:66,459] The Office of the State Fire Marshal (OSFM) is comprised of the following six divisions:  

[10:0:65,477] • Code Development and Analysis: The Code Development and Analysis division is responsible for the development and  

[10:1:76,487] adoption of codes relating to fire and life safety used statewide by architects, engineers, design professionals, and the local  

[10:2:76,498] fire and building authorities. This division fosters, promotes, and develops ways and means of protecting life and property  

[10:3:76,508] against fire in many ways, including the adoption and implementation of regulations for statewide application. This division  

[10:4:76,518] prepares the OSFM fire and life safety regulations and building standards for review and adoption into the California Code of  

[10:5:76,528] Regulations, titles 19 and 24.   The division assists local fire departments, fire districts, and building departments with the  

[10:6:76,538] application of state laws and regulations.   This division also reviews all of California's regulations relating to fire and life  

[10:7:76,549] safety for relevancy, necessity, conflict, duplication, and overlap.  

[11:0:65,565] • Fire and Life Safety: The Fire and Life Safety division is responsible for applying laws and regulations related to fire  

[11:1:76,575] prevention and life safety in state-owned and state-occupied facilities. This is achieved through code compliance inspections  

[11:2:76,585] of new and existing buildings and plan review and construction inspections. The OSFM is responsible for fire and life safety  

[11:3:76,595] in over 45,000 state-owned and state-occupied facilities, which include: state prisons, conservation camps, courts, state  

[11:4:76,606] mental hospitals, state developmental centers, California State University and University of California campuses, and  

[11:5:76,616] California Agricultural Districts.  

[12:0:65,632] • Fire Engineering and Investigations: The Fire Engineering and Investigations division’s primary functions include licensing,  

[12:1:76,642] product approval, fire/explosion investigations, and illegal fireworks enforcement and disposal. The division is responsible for  

[12:2:76,652] 11 statewide programs, which include the Building Materials Listing; Portable Fire Extinguishers; Flame Retardant Chemicals  

[12:3:76,663] and Fabrics; Vapor Recovery; Automatic Fire Extinguishing Systems; Fire Safe Cigarettes; Licensing Enforcement and  

[12:4:76,673] Inspection; Lab Accreditation; Motion Picture and Entertainment; Arson and Bomb Investigation; and Fireworks Disposal.  

[12:5:76,683] This division also oversees the Arson and Bomb Unit, which has the responsibility to dispose of seized illegal fireworks and  

[12:6:76,693] conduct fire/explosion investigations and licensing enforcement.  

[13:0:65,709] • Pipeline Safety: The Pipeline Safety division ensures the safe construction, operation, and maintenance of approximately  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:129,32] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection - Continued 

[3:0:76,69] 5,400 miles of intrastate hazardous liquid pipelines that transport crude oil, refined products (e.g., gasoline, diesel, jet fuel,  

[3:1:76,79] etc.), and Highly Volatile Liquids through populated urban areas, ecologically sensitive areas, and other high consequence  

[3:2:76,89] areas. Pipelines that fall under the Pipeline Safety division authority are pipelines that transport hazardous liquids between  

[3:3:76,99] facilities, such as offshore platforms, production fields, refineries, storage terminals, and marine terminals. This division is  

[3:4:76,109] additionally charged with implementing investigations for failures, explosions, and fires on intrastate pipelines, and assisting  

[3:5:76,120] on investigations for interstate pipelines.   The Certified Unified Program Agencies program is responsible for ensuring the  

[3:6:76,130] implementation of the California Fire Code Hazardous Materials Management Plan/Hazardous Materials Inventory  

[3:7:76,140] Statement (HMMP/HMIS) and the Aboveground Petroleum Storage Act program elements.   The HMMP/HMIS program ties  

[3:8:76,150] in closely with the Hazardous Release Response Plan and Inventory or Hazardous Materials Business Plan program.   The  

[3:9:76,160] Firefighter Equipment and Foam program is tasked to monitor the use of Perfluoroalky and Polyfluoroalkyl substances  

[3:10:76,171] (PFAS) throughout the state at designated facilities, issue waivers to refineries and terminals for fixed suppression systems  

[3:11:76,181] meeting identified criteria.  

[4:0:65,197] • State Fire Training: The State Fire Training division administers the California Fire Service Training and Education System  

[4:1:76,207] and the Fire Service Training and Education Program for federal, state, and local firefighters. The division oversees a  

[4:2:76,217] California Fire Academy System for over 65 training academies that represent a partnership with the fire departments,  

[4:3:76,228] community colleges, and the OSFM. State Fire Training administers a professional certification system for fire service  

[4:4:76,238] personnel and receives program guidance from the State Board of Fire Services.  

[5:0:65,254] • Wildfire Planning and Engineering: The Wildfire Planning and Engineering division will be transitioning into the new  

[6:0:76,264] Community Wildfire Preparedness and Mitigation Division established by the passage of Chapter 225, Statutes of 2021 (AB  

[6:1:76,274] 9). The division is responsible for various community-level fire prevention and preparedness programs including defensible  

[6:2:76,285] space, home hardening, fire planning, land use planning, utility fire mitigation, fire prevention grants, fire hazard mapping,  

[6:3:76,295] incident data and statistics, and damage inspection.  

[7:0:66,313] 2461019 - California Underground Facilities Safe Excavation Board:  

[8:0:66,330] Also called the “Dig Safe Board,” created by the passage of Chapter 809, Statutes of 2016 (SB 661), the Board works on  

[8:1:66,340] issues related to the state's "Call Before You Dig" law, which requires excavators and buried infrastructure owners to coordinate  

[8:2:66,351] to prevent infrastructure damage and unsafe outcomes.   The Dig Safe Board is charged with coordinating the state’s safe  

[8:3:66,361] excavation outreach and education efforts, developing safe excavation standards, investigating accidents and working with  

[8:4:66,371] partner state agencies to enforce the “Call Before You Dig” law.   The Dig Safe Board was transferred to the Office of Energy  

[8:5:66,381] Infrastructure Safety on January 1, 2022 to comply with the requirements of Chapter 307, Statutes of 2020 (SB 865).  

[9:0:66,399] 2465 - FIRE PROTECTION  

[10:0:66,417] CAL FIRE provides fire protection to keep damages to life, property, and natural resources within social, political, and economic  

[10:1:66,427] constraints.   The objective is to attack fires quickly and aggressively in areas where CAL FIRE has assumed primary direct  

[10:2:66,437] protection responsibility by virtue of law, contract, or agreement until the fire is under control.   The level of initial attack and  

[10:3:66,447] follow-up action is relative to values threatened and control difficulty with the goal to control all unwanted fires within the first  

[10:4:66,457] burning period.  

[11:0:66,475] 2465010 - Fire Prevention:  

[12:0:66,493] This program is responsible for implementation of the Strategic Fire Plan, fire engineering, law enforcement, fire suppression  

[12:1:66,503] cost recovery, and fire prevention education.   This program focuses on the most effective methods, materials, and procedures  

[12:2:66,513] to mitigate hazard and to enforce pertinent laws that deter and mitigate fires.   More specifically, efforts focus on what needs to  

[12:3:66,523] be done before a wildland fire starts to reduce the costs of firefighting, property loss, injury to the public and fire fighters, and  

[12:4:66,534] damage to the environment.  

[13:0:66,551] 2465019 - Fire Control:  

[14:0:66,569] The objective of this program is to detect, respond to, and suppress wildland fires in or threatening the State Responsibility  

[14:1:66,579] Area.   The heart of the effort is an aggressive initial attack strategy.   CAL FIRE's goal is to contain 95 percent of all wildfires to  

[14:2:66,589] 10 acres or less.   This is achieved through detection, ground attack, air attack, and mutual aid using fire engines, fire crews,  

[14:3:66,600] bulldozers, helicopters, and fixed wing aircraft.  

[15:0:66,617] 2465028 - Cooperative Fire Protection:  

[16:0:66,635] CAL FIRE provides a full range of fire protection services to local, county, state, and federal agencies throughout California  

[16:1:66,645] through the administration of over 100 cooperative fire protection service agreements.   Additionally, there are six long standing  

[16:2:66,655] agreements wherein counties provide wildland fire protection on behalf of the Department.  

[17:0:66,673] 2465037 - Conservation Camps:  

[18:0:66,691] CAL FIRE, in cooperation with the Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation, Divisions of Adult Parole Operations and  

[18:1:66,701] Division of Juvenile Justice, operates 30 conservation camps throughout the state with capacity to house 152 fire crews.   CAL  

[18:2:66,711] FIRE operates seven permanent Fire Centers, six seasonal Fire Centers, and one Parole Training Center with the California  

[19:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] Conservation Corps throughout the state that house 28 fire crews.   CAL FIRE also operates in conjunction with the California  

[3:1:66,79] National Guard to staff seven Fire Centers with a total of 10 fire crews.   Additionally, CAL FIRE is currently authorized to  

[3:2:66,89] operate 16 CAL FIRE Firefighter Crews located at seven different Fire Centers.   These crews are available to respond to all  

[3:3:66,99] types of emergencies including wildfires, floods, search and rescue, and earthquakes.   When not responding to emergencies,  

[3:4:66,109] the crews engage in hazardous fuels reduction, conservation related work projects, and prescribed fire project work to protect  

[3:5:66,120] the community and resources of California.  

[4:0:66,137] 2465046 - Emergency Fire Suppression:  

[5:0:66,155] CAL FIRE incurs additional emergency fire suppression expenditures when budgeted initial attack forces are unable to cope  

[5:1:66,165] with a wildland fire within the initial attack period.   Portions of these costs may be reimbursed in the fiscal year the expense was  

[5:2:66,175] incurred or in a future fiscal year via Fire Management Assistance Grants provided by the Federal Emergency Management  

[5:3:66,186] Agency or through cost shares provided by other government entities.   In addition, CAL FIRE may recover portions of this cost  

[5:4:66,196] through civil cost recovery actions authorized by state statutes.  

[6:0:66,214] 2470 - RESOURCE MANAGEMENT  

[7:0:66,231] California's state and private forest, range, watershed lands, and urban forests provide multiple human, climate, and  

[8:0:66,241] environmental benefits.   The objective of this program is to maintain and enhance those benefits and to minimize damage to  

[8:1:66,252] these resources from natural catastrophes and human development.   Objectives are met by regulation of timber harvesting,  

[8:2:66,262] coordination of climate and forest restoration related activities for the forest sector, technical assistance to non-industrial  

[8:3:66,272] landowners, operation of state demonstration forests, operation of forest nurseries, vegetation management projects, and  

[8:4:66,282] administration of federal forestry assistance programs.  

[9:0:66,300] 2470010 - Resources Protection and Improvement:  

[10:0:66,318] The objective of this program is to improve forest lands and urban forests.   Activities include: (1) forest restoration through fuels  

[10:1:66,328] reduction and fire reintroduction; (2) the detection, evaluation and control of forest pests; (3) growing and selling tree seedlings  

[10:2:66,338] for reforestation, carbon sequestration through reforestation, biomass utilization, and wood products development; (4) avoided  

[10:3:66,348] future emissions through fuels and fire behavior modification; (5) soil erosion control; (6) maintenance of a native conifer seed  

[10:4:66,358] bank; (7) advice and assistance to non-industrial forest landowners on management of forests and improved harvesting and  

[10:5:66,369] conservation practices; (8) research on and demonstration of sustainable forestry in state forests; (9) implementation of the  

[10:6:66,379] California Forest Improvement Act of 1978; (10) technical assistance and grants to foster the environmental, social, and  

[11:0:66,389] economic benefits of urban forests consistent with the California Urban Forestry Act of 1978; and (11) purchase and monitoring  

[11:1:66,399] of working forest conservation easements per the California Forest Legacy Act of 2008.   In the area of vegetation management,  

[11:2:66,409] assistance is provided to public and private landowners to achieve land use objectives by reducing damage from wildland fires,  

[11:3:66,420] increase wildlife habitat, increase productivity of forest and rangelands, improve water yields and air quality, reduce carbon  

[11:4:66,430] emissions from wildland fires, increase firefighter safety, and maintain desirable ecosystems. 

[12:0:66,448] CAL   FIRE cooperates with federal, state and local agencies, local organizations, and private property owners to develop and  

[12:1:66,458] achieve land use objectives.   Activities include removal, rearrangement, conversion, or improvement of vegetation using  

[12:2:66,468] various treatment measures such as prescribed fire and mechanical, manual, and biological methods.   The Environmental  

[12:3:66,478] Protection Program provides in-house expertise and oversight of the Department's compliance with the California  

[13:0:66,488] Environmental Quality Act, the National Environmental Policy Act, and other environmental laws and regulations applicable to  

[13:1:66,499] departmental actions.   The Climate and Energy program represents the Department in Natural and Working Lands and Climate  

[13:2:66,509] Change Scoping Plan planning.  

[14:0:66,526] 2470019 - Forest Practice Regulations:  

[15:0:66,544] The objective of this program is to maximize sustained production of high-quality timber products while protecting soil, water,  

[15:1:66,554] wildlife, recreation, and other values associated with forest land.   Forest Practice activities include the review and permitting of  

[15:2:66,565] commercial timber harvesting operations on nonfederal timberlands, enforcing state forest practice laws and regulations, and  

[15:3:66,575] monitoring compliance and the effects of timber operations on forest productivity, water quality, watersheds, soils, botanical  

[15:4:66,585] resources, wildlife, fisheries, greenhouse gases, and other environmentally sensitive resources.   The program is also  

[16:0:66,595] responsible for issuing licenses to timber operators (LTOs), providing LTO training and education, and assisting the Board of  

[16:1:66,605] Equalization-Timber Tax Division in their administration of taxes on timber and timberland.   Forest Practice program staff also  

[16:2:66,616] assist the Board of Forestry and Fire Protection, and facilitate and participate in monitoring the effectiveness of the Board's  

[16:3:66,626] forest practice regulations.  

[17:0:66,643] 2470028 - Forest Resource Inventory and Assessment:  

[18:0:66,661] The objective of this program is to perform a periodic assessment of California's forest and rangeland resources.   Activities  

[18:1:66,671] include maintaining and distributing mission-critical datasets for fire planning, emergency incident support, and meeting certain  

[18:2:66,682] regulatory requirements.   In addition, the program provides extensive technical and public information for statewide fire threat,  

[18:3:66,692] fire hazard, watersheds, socio-economic conditions, environmental indicators, and urban forestry.  

[19:0:66,709] 2470037 - Professional Foresters Registration Program:

[3:4:66,117] 2475 - BOARD OF FORESTRY AND FIRE PROTECTION  

[4:0:66,135] The Board of Forestry and Fire Protection is a Governor-appointed body within CAL FIRE. The Board is responsible for  

[4:1:66,145] developing the general forest policy of the state, determining the guidance policies of CAL FIRE, and representing the state's  

[4:2:66,155] interest in federal forests in California. Together, the Board and CAL FIRE work to carry out statutory mandates to protect and  

[4:3:66,165] enhance the state's unique forest and wildland resources. The Board's statutory responsibilities are to:  

[4:4:65,183] • Establish and administer forest and rangeland policy for the State of California;  

[4:5:65,199] • Protect and represent the state's interest in all forestry and rangeland matters;  

[5:0:65,215] • Provide direction and guidance to the Director of CAL FIRE on fire protection and resource management;  

[5:1:65,232] • Deliver a comprehensive regulatory program for forestry and fire protection; and  

[5:2:65,248] • Conduct its duties to inform and respond to the people of California.  

[5:3:66,265] 2480 - DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE LEGAL SERVICES  

[6:0:66,283] The objective of the Department of Justice Legal Services program is to account for the cost of legal services provided by the  

[6:1:66,293] Department of Justice to CAL FIRE.  

[6:2:66,311] 9900100 - ADMINISTRATION  

[7:0:66,329] Departmental administration provides executive leadership, policy direction, and a variety of program support services  

[7:1:66,339] necessary for the successful performance of CAL   FIRE's mission.   CAL FIRE headquarters provides leadership through the  

[7:2:66,349] executive office and through central services in accounting, budgeting, business services, legal, human resources, information  

[7:3:66,359] technology, program accountability, and program and systems analysis.   CAL FIRE field units provide localized general support  

[7:4:66,370] services throughout the state.

[6:0:66,208] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[7:0:66,227] The Department of Forestry and Fire Protection (CAL FIRE) operates more than 530 facilities statewide. These facilities  

[7:1:66,237] include, but are not limited to, 234 fire stations, 112 telecommunications facilities, 31 conservation camps, 8 fire crew camps,  

[7:2:66,247] 21 unit headquarters, 16 administrative headquarters, 13 air attack bases, 10 helitack bases, 8 state forests, 1 forestry nursery,  

[7:3:66,257] and 3 training centers. CAL FIRE facilities support fire protection and resource   management efforts for more than 31 million  

[7:4:66,268] acres of both state and privately-owned wildlands throughout California.

[7:0:215,393] 3560   State Lands Commission  

[8:0:66,415] The California State Lands Commission provides effective stewardship of the lands, waterways, and resources entrusted to its  

[8:1:66,425] care through preservation, restoration, enhancement, responsible economic development, and the promotion of public access.  

[8:2:66,435] Execution of the Commission's responsibilities since its inception in 1938 has resulted in the generation of billions of dollars   in  

[8:3:66,445] revenues, while protecting and enhancing the public's ability to enjoy these state lands and resources.  

[9:0:66,463] Consisting of the Lieutenant Governor, the State Controller, and the Governor's Director of Finance, the California State Lands  

[9:1:66,473] Commission serves as a trustee for the people of the state, managing approximately 4 million acres of California's sovereign  

[9:2:66,483] public trust lands and resources, received upon admission into the Union in 1850. It also manages other lands that the federal  

[9:3:66,494] government conveyed to the state (commonly known as school lands) and oversees the management of public trust lands  

[9:4:66,504] legislatively granted in trust to over 70 local jurisdictions. These trust grants encourage responsible development,  

[9:5:66,514] environmental preservation and enhancement, and public access on the state's tidelands, consistent with the common law  

[9:6:66,524] Public Trust Doctrine. The grants typically require grantees to reinvest revenues generated from the granted lands back into the  

[9:7:66,534] trust to the benefit of all Californians.  

[10:0:66,552] Public trust, or sovereign lands, include the beds of natural and navigable waterways, including non-tidal rivers, streams and  

[10:1:66,562] lakes, and tide and submerged lands within tidal rivers, sloughs, bays, and the Pacific Ocean extending from the ordinary high  

[10:2:66,573] water mark seaward to the three-mile offshore limit. In addition to sovereign lands, the Commission manages school lands,  

[10:3:66,583] which were granted to California by the federal government in 1853 for the purpose of supporting public education in California.  

[10:4:66,593] School lands typically include the 16th and 36th sections of each township and lands known to be mineral in character. Of the  

[10:5:66,603] five and one-half million acres of school lands originally granted to the state, only about 458,843 acres remain in state  

[10:6:66,613] ownership, and these are mostly concentrated in the California desert. The Commission also retains a reserved mineral interest  

[10:7:66,624] in approximately 790,000 acres of sold school lands.  

[11:0:66,641] Because the State Lands Commission’s (SLC) programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, SLC has a capital outlay  

[11:1:66,651] program to support this need. For the specifics on the SLC's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."  

[12:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:0:66,377] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,397] 2560 - MINERAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[7:0:66,414] The   Mineral Resources Management Division   oversees the efficient and safe development of mineral resources on state lands.  

[7:1:66,425] The state also has a beneficial interest in the Long Beach tidelands oil fields, and as such, the Commission participates in the  

[7:2:66,435] safety and fiscal oversight of these fields. The program objectives are to: manage the responsible extraction of oil, gas,  

[7:3:66,445] geothermal resources and other solid minerals; collect royalty and other revenues generated therefrom; and ensure that public  

[7:4:66,455] safety and environmental protection are maintained at the highest possible standards in the development of these resources  

[7:5:66,465] through engineering review and safety audits. The Commission also implements various programs for the prevention of oil  

[7:6:66,476] spills and the safe operation of offshore facilities and   conducts program and financial audits to ensure that the state receives  

[7:7:66,486] fair value for the development of its resources. The Commission also manages the safe and effective decommissioning of the  

[7:8:66,496] oil and gas facilities under its jurisdiction.  

[8:0:66,514] Further, the Commission manages the mineral resources associated with school lands under its purview, including revenue-

[8:1:66,524] generating extraction and prospecting leases and other projects. The Commission also coordinates with other agencies to  

[8:2:66,534] administer the statewide Abandoned Mines Program.  

[9:0:66,552] 2565 - LAND MANAGEMENT DIVISION  

[10:0:66,569] The Land Management Division manages approximately 4 million acres of tide and submerged lands and the beds of natural  

[10:1:66,580] and navigable rivers, streams, lakes, bays, estuaries, inlets, and straits. These lands, often referred to as sovereign or Public  

[10:2:66,590] Trust lands, stretch from the Klamath River and Goose Lake in the north to the Tijuana Estuary in the south, and from   Lake  

[10:3:66,600] Tahoe and   the Colorado River in the east, to three miles offshore from the Pacific Coast   in the west, and include California’s  

[10:4:66,610] two longest rivers, the Sacramento and San Joaquin.  

[11:0:66,628] The Commission also monitors sovereign land granted in trust by the California Legislature to approximately 70 local  

[11:1:66,638] jurisdictions that generally consist of prime waterfront lands and coastal waters, including the state’s five major ports. The  

[11:2:66,648] Commission protects and enhances these lands and natural resources by issuing leases for use or development, preserving  

[11:3:66,659] and expanding public access, and resolving boundaries between public and private lands.  

[12:0:66,676] Finally, the Commission manages the state’s school lands, which seeks to develop and consolidate the state’s school-land  

[12:1:66,686] holdings to the economic benefit of the California State Teachers’ Retirement System.  

[13:0:66,704] 2570 - MARINE ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION DIVISION  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:180,32] 3560   State Lands Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The   Marine Environmental Protection Division   regulates marine oil terminals within California, providing the best achievable  

[3:1:66,79] protection of the public health and safety and the environment. The Division monitors the transfer of oil at marine terminals,  

[3:2:66,89] inspects all marine facilities, and reviews and approves all marine oil terminal operations manuals and training and certification  

[3:3:66,99] programs. The Division also administers Chapter 31F of the California Building Code to ensure that marine oil terminals are fit  

[3:4:66,109] for their purpose, and implements the state's Marine Invasive Species Program to prevent the introduction of nonindigenous  

[3:5:66,120] species into California waters through the operations of oceangoing vessels. The program funds and conducts scientific  

[3:6:66,130] research, develops rules and regulations to implement the Marine Invasive Species Act, and physically inspects oceangoing  

[3:7:66,140] vessels to assess compliance with vessel-reported nonindigenous species management actions.  

[3:8:66,158] 9900100 - EXECUTIVE AND ADMINISTRATION  

[4:0:66,175] The Executive and Administration program provides management, policy direction, and administrative support to the  

[4:1:66,186] Commission’s line programs.

[6:0:66,208] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[7:0:66,227] The California State Lands Commission operates five facilities statewide. Two are regional headquarters, each co-located with  

[7:1:66,237] a field office, one located in Sacramento and the other in Long Beach. The three remaining facilities are field offices only, one in  

[7:2:66,247] Northern California and two in Southern California. Of the five facilities, one is state-owned (Huntington Beach Field Office),  

[7:3:66,257] and the other four are in leased space.

[15:0:197,649] 3600   Department of Fish and Wildlife  

[15:1:66,670] The mission of the Department of Fish and Wildlife is to manage California's diverse fish, wildlife, and plant resources, and the  

[15:2:66,680] habitats upon which they depend, for their ecological value and for their use and enjoyment by the public.  

[16:0:66,701] This includes habitat protection and maintenance in a sufficient amount and quality to protect the survival of species and  

[16:1:66,711] natural communities. The Department is also responsible for the diversified use of fish and wildlife including recreational,
