[3:0:66,69] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[4:0:65,88] • Climate Resilience Package—The Budget includes $35 million General Fund in 2022-23, as part of a $3.7 billion package of  

[4:1:76,98] investments that address the state’s climate risks. This includes funding to address climate change impacts on fish and  

[4:2:76,109] wildlife.  

[5:0:65,125] • Drought Resilience and Response Package—The Budget includes $75 million California Emergency Relief Fund in 2021-22,  

[5:1:76,135] and $3.3 million General Fund in 2022-23, as part of a $2.7 billion package of investments to support the state’s response to  

[5:2:76,145] continued drought conditions. This includes funding to mitigate immediate drought impacts on fish and wildlife, including  

[5:3:76,155] habitat restoration and monitoring, hatchery and refuge improvements, increased salmon production, fish passage above rim  

[5:4:76,166] dams, and wildlife rescue and relief, as well as tribal co-management activities. Of the $2.7 billion, $1.5 billion will be  

[5:5:76,176] allocated at a later time.  

[5:6:65,192] • Wildfire and Forest Resilience Package—The Budget includes $30 million General Fund in 2022-23, as part of a $1.2 billion  

[5:7:76,202] package of investments that target the state’s wildfire risks. This includes funding to support the stewardship of state-owned  

[5:8:76,212] land. Of the $1.2 billion, $530 million will be allocated at a later time.

[5:0:66,509] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,528] 2590 - BIODIVERSITY CONSERVATION PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,548] This program encourages the preservation, conservation, maintenance, and restoration of wildlife resources, including the  

[6:1:66,559] Ecosystem Restoration Program, under the jurisdiction and influence of the state. Activities involve the conservation, protection  

[6:2:66,569] and management of fish, wildlife, native plants, and habitat to ensure maintenance of biologically sustainable populations of  

[6:3:66,579] those species.  

[7:0:66,597] 2595 - HUNTING, FISHING, AND PUBLIC USE PROGRAM  

[8:0:66,614] This program facilitates diverse and sustainable hunting, fishing (recreational and commercial), trapping, and other public uses  

[8:1:66,625] and associated economic benefits to the state by conserving and managing game species. Activities include collection and  

[8:2:66,635] assessment of information on the distribution and abundance of game fish and wildlife to determine appropriate regulations  

[8:3:66,645] (bag limits, gear restrictions, etc.) and to monitor the effects of those regulations.  

[8:4:66,663] 2600 - MANAGEMENT OF DEPARTMENT LANDS AND FACILITIES PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,680] This program manages Department-owned or leased lands and facilities, including hatcheries, wildlife areas, ecological  

[9:1:66,691] reserves, fish and wildlife laboratories, and public access areas, to contribute to the conservation, protection, and management  

[9:2:66,701] of fish and wildlife.  

[10:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] 2605 - ENFORCEMENT  

[4:0:66,89] This program serves the public through law enforcement, public safety and hunter education. Law enforcement promotes  

[4:1:66,99] compliance with laws and regulations protecting fish and wildlife resources; and investigates habitat destruction, pollution  

[4:2:66,109] incidents and illegal commercialization of wildlife. Wardens also serve the public through general law enforcement, mutual aid  

[4:3:66,120] and homeland security.  

[5:0:66,137] 2610 - COMMUNICATIONS, EDUCATION, AND OUTREACH PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,158] This program provides resource conservation education and activities in the classroom and on public and private lands;  

[6:1:66,168] community and stakeholder outreach; and information and data using a variety of methods including publications,  

[6:2:66,178] presentations, web applications, and media relations.  

[6:3:66,196] 2615 - SPILL PREVENTION AND RESPONSE PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,216] This program minimizes damage and environmental impacts to, restores, and rehabilitates California's fish and wildlife  

[7:1:66,226] populations and their habitats from the harmful effects of oil and other deleterious material spills in marine waters and inland  

[7:2:66,237] habitats.  

[8:0:66,254] 2620 - FISH AND GAME COMMISSION  

[9:0:66,272] The California Fish and Game Commission ensures the long term sustainability of California's fish and wildlife resources by  

[9:1:66,282] guiding the ongoing scientific evaluation and assessment of California's fish and wildlife resources; setting California's fish and  

[9:2:66,292] wildlife resource management policies and ensuring these are implemented by the Department of Fish and Wildlife;  

[9:3:66,303] establishing appropriate fish and wildlife resource management rules and regulations; and building active fish and wildlife  

[9:4:66,313] resource management partnerships with individual landowners, the public and interest groups, and federal, state and local  

[9:5:66,323] resource management agencies.

[9:0:66,407] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[10:0:66,426] The Department of Fish and Wildlife manages more than 740 properties statewide, comprising more than 1 million acres  

[10:1:66,436] (approximately 679,000 acres of state-owned land and 484,000 acres owned by other entities, but managed by the  

[10:2:66,447] Department. Several state agencies purchase land for the purpose of habitat or wildlife protection, and management  

[10:3:66,457] responsibilities for these properties are often transferred to the Department; therefore, the amount of land under Department  

[10:4:66,467] control continues to increase. Examples of the properties managed by the Department include, and are not limited to: 111  

[10:5:66,477] wildlife areas, 136 ecological reserves, 138 public access areas, and 21 fish hatcheries.

[5:0:66,126] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[5:1:65,145] • Climate Resilience Package—The Budget includes $222 million General Fund in 2022-23, and $100 million General Fund in  

[5:2:76,156] 2023-24, as part of a $3.7 billion package that address the state’s climate risks. This includes funding to protect fish and  

[5:3:76,166] wildlife from changing conditions.  

[5:4:65,182] • Water Resilience and Drought Package—The Budget includes $190 million General Fund in 2022-23, as part of a $5.2  

[5:5:76,192] billion package to support water resilience and drought response efforts. This includes funding to support wildlife corridors,  

[5:6:76,202] fish passage, and the streamflow enhancement program.

[5:0:66,167] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,187] 2710 - WILDLIFE CONSERVATION BOARD  

[6:0:66,204] The Wildlife Conservation Board's three main functions are: (1) land acquisition; (2) habitat protection, restoration, and  

[6:1:66,214] enhancement, including conservation of forests, wetlands, riparian habitat, oak woodlands and protection of rangeland, grazing  

[6:2:66,225] land and grasslands to support biodiversity and climate change adaptation; and (3) development of wildlife-oriented public  

[6:3:66,235] access and recreational areas.

[4:0:198,122] 3720   California Coastal Commission  

[5:0:66,144] The California Coastal Commission, comprised of 12 voting members appointed by the Governor, the Senate Rules  

[5:1:66,154] Committee, and the Speaker of the Assembly, was created by voter initiative in 1972 and was made permanent by the  

[5:2:66,164] California Coastal Act of 1976 (Coastal Act). The Coastal Act calls for the protection and enhancement of public access and  

[5:3:66,174] recreation, marine resources, environmentally sensitive habitat areas, marine water quality, agriculture, and scenic resources;  

[5:4:66,185] and makes provisions for coastal-dependent industrial and energy development. The Commission is also designated the  

[5:5:66,195] principal state coastal management agency for the purpose of administering the federal Coastal Zone Management Act in  

[5:6:66,205] California and has exclusive regulatory authority over federal activities such as permits, leases, federal development projects,  

[5:7:66,215] and other federal actions that could affect coastal zone resources and that would not otherwise be subject to state control.

[6:0:66,418] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:1:66,438] 2730 - COASTAL MANAGEMENT PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,458] The objectives of the Coastal Management Program are to implement coastal resources conservation through planning and  

[7:1:66,468] regulation. Activities include:  

[8:0:65,486] • Reviewing and approval of local coastal programs (LCPs), port master plans, university long-range development plans, and  

[8:1:76,496] any amendments to such plans, for consistency with the Coastal Act.  

[9:0:65,512] • Reviewing coastal development permit applications for new development in areas without a certified LCP, areas of  

[9:1:76,523] permanently retained jurisdiction (e.g., tidelands, submerged lands, and public trust lands) and limited categories of local  

[9:2:76,533] coastal development permit actions that can be appealed to the Commission.  

[9:3:65,549] • Monitoring and enforcement of coastal development permits.  

[9:4:65,565] • Reviewing federal activities for consistency with the Coastal Act.  

[9:5:65,581] • Protecting and expanding opportunities for public coastal access and recreation.  

[9:6:65,598] • Implementing a coastal water quality protection program.  

[9:7:65,614] • Providing technical information and assistance to support effective coastal management.  

[9:8:65,630] • Implementing a coastal and ocean resource public education program.  

[10:0:65,646] • Addressing coastal energy issues including, but not limited to, offshore oil and gas development, offshore wind projects,  

[10:1:76,656] electricity generating power plant expansion and development, and siting and development of liquefied natural gas facilities.  

[10:2:65,673] • Providing administrative support including accounting, budgeting, business services, support services, information  

[10:3:76,683] technology, and human resources services.  

[11:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[13:0:210,647] 3760   State Coastal Conservancy  

[14:0:66,668] The State Coastal Conservancy develops and implements projects to protect, restore, and enhance natural, recreational, and  

[14:1:66,678] economic resources along California's coast, coastal watersheds, the ocean, and within the San Francisco Bay Area. The  

[14:2:66,689] Coastal Conservancy supports implementation of the San Francisco Bay Area Conservancy Program and the Santa Ana River  

[14:3:66,699] Conservancy Program. Through technical assistance, direct funding, and grants to local agencies and nonprofit organizations,  

[14:4:66,709] the Coastal Conservancy supports projects that:  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:175,32] 3760   State Coastal Conservancy - Continued 

[3:0:65,69] • Protect and enhance the natural environment, agricultural lands, scenic lands, and urban waterfronts along the Coast and  

[3:1:76,79] around San Francisco Bay.  

[4:0:65,95] • Increase public access to the coast and San Francisco Bay.  

[4:1:65,111] • Build trails and increase outdoor recreational opportunities.

[7:0:66,187] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[7:1:65,207] • Wildfire and Forest Resilience Package—The Budget includes $10 million General Fund in 2022-23 for stewardship of state  

[7:2:76,217] owned land as part of a $1.2 billion package of investments that target the state’s wildfire risks. Of the total $1.2 billion, $730  

[7:3:76,227] million will be allocated at a later time.  

[7:4:65,243] • Sea Level Rise Adaptation—The Budget includes $40 million General Fund and $80 million Greenhouse Gas Reduction  

[7:5:76,253] Fund to address sea level rise. An additional $300 million General Fund one-time appropriation will be included in 2023-24.  

[7:6:65,270] • Climate Resilience Package—The budget includes $350 million General Fund for grants or expenditures for the protection  

[7:7:76,280] and restoration of coastal and ocean resources from climate change impacts such as sea level rise originally included as a  

[7:8:76,290] set aside in Chapter 258 Statutes of 2021 (SB 155).   An additional $150 million General Fund one-time appropriation will be  

[7:9:76,300] included in 2023-24.

[5:0:66,317] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,337] 2790 - COASTAL CONSERVANCY PROGRAM  

[5:2:66,354] The Coastal Conservancy seeks to achieve the following goals:  

[6:0:65,372] • Protect and enhance habitats, wetlands, scenic open space, working lands and urban waterfronts along the California coast  

[6:1:76,382] and around San Francisco Bay.  

[7:0:65,398] • Increase public access to the coast and outdoor recreational opportunities by creating new parks, trails and recreational  

[7:1:76,409] amenities.  

[8:0:65,425] • Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area.  

[8:1:65,441] • Support education projects and programs related to the preservation, enhancement, and enjoyment of coastal resources.  

[8:2:65,457] • Facilitate improvement of existing, and the development of new, lower cost accommodations within one and one-half miles of  

[8:3:76,467] the coast.  

[9:0:66,485] 2805 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY LOCAL ASSISTANCE PROGRAM  

[10:0:66,506] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to local governments, nonprofits and other project  

[10:1:66,516] partners for the acquisition of land and easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The State  

[10:2:66,526] Coastal Conservancy helps to implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program and supports programs  

[10:3:66,536] that increase public access to the coast. The Conservancy has participated in the protection of more than 390,000 acres of  

[10:4:66,546] coastal lands through acquisition of fee title and conservation easements. The Conservancy has helped restore more than  

[10:5:66,557] 33,000 acres of habitat.  

[11:0:66,577] Through its projects, the Conservancy has established approximately 160 new coastal accessways and built more than 168  

[11:1:66,587] miles of new trails.  

[12:0:66,605] State Coastal Conservancy funds are primarily expended for purposes of local assistance but may also be expended for capital  

[12:1:66,615] outlay purposes, see Program 2820.  

[12:2:66,633] 2820 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY CAPITAL OUTLAY PROGRAM  

[12:3:66,650] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to state agencies for the acquisition of land and  

[12:4:66,661] easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The State Coastal Conservancy helps to  

[12:5:66,671] implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program. The Conservancy has participated in the protection of  

[12:6:66,681] more than 390,000 acres of coastal lands through acquisition of fee title and conservation easements and the Conservancy has  

[12:7:66,691] helped restore more than 33,000 acres of habitat.  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[7:0:183,358] 3790   Department of Parks and Recreation  

[8:0:66,379] The mission of the California Department of Parks and Recreation (Parks) is to provide for the health, inspiration, and  

[8:1:66,389] education of the people of California by helping to preserve the state's extraordinary biological diversity, protecting its most  

[8:2:66,400] valued natural, cultural and historical resources, and creating opportunities for high-quality outdoor recreation for current and  

[8:3:66,410] future generations to enjoy. With increased urbanization, the establishment of park units and recreation areas accessible to the  

[8:4:66,420] major population centers of the state has become particularly important. Specific activities include: stewardship of natural  

[8:5:66,430] resources, historic, cultural and archeological sites, and artifacts and structures; provision of interpretive services for park  

[8:6:66,440] visitors; construction and maintenance of campsites, trails, visitor centers, museums, and infrastructure such as roads and  

[8:7:66,451] water systems; and creation of recreational opportunities such as hiking, bicycling, fishing, swimming, horseback riding,  

[8:8:66,461] jogging, camping, picnicking, and off-highway vehicle recreation. In addition, the Division of Boating and Waterways funds,  

[8:9:66,471] plans, and develops boating facilities on waterways throughout California and ensures safe boating for the public by providing  

[8:10:66,481] financial aid and training to local law enforcement agencies.  

[9:0:66,502] Because Parks' programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, Parks has a capital outlay program to support this need.  

[9:1:66,512] For the specifics on Parks' capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[5:0:177,266] 3810   Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy  

[5:1:66,287] The Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy acquires, restores, and consolidates lands in the Santa Monica Mountains, Rim of  

[5:2:66,298] the Valley Trail Corridor, Upper Los Angeles River Watershed, and watersheds of Santa Monica Bay for park, recreation, and  

[5:3:66,308] conservation purposes. The Conservancy may:  

[5:4:65,325] • Lease, rent, sell, transfer, or exchange lands for park purposes.  

[6:0:65,342] • Award grants or interest free loans to state and local agencies to purchase or restore park, recreation, conservation, or  

[6:1:76,352] buffer-zone areas to ensure that the character and intensity of development on these lands is generally compatible with and  

[6:2:76,362] does not adversely impact the environment.  

[7:0:65,378] • Implement programs to improve access from the inner city areas surrounding the Conservancy, thereby providing  

[7:1:76,388] recreational opportunities for all residents.  

[7:2:65,405] • Manage the Rim of the Valley Trail Corridor recreational trail.  

[8:0:65,421] • Plans for restoration, recreation, and conservation projects for the Los Angeles River and Upper Los Angeles River  

[8:1:76,431] Watershed.

[11:0:104,621] 3820   San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission  

[12:0:66,642] The San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission is a state planning and regulatory agency with regional  

[12:1:66,653] authority. Its mission is to protect and enhance the   San Francisco Bay, and to encourage the Bay's responsible and productive  

[12:2:66,663] use. The Commission authored and maintains the San Francisco Bay Plan, and relies on the McAteer-Petris Act and other  

[12:3:66,673] statutory and regulatory authorities to maximize public access to the Bay and minimize Bay fill. The Commission issues permits  

[12:4:66,683] for filling, dredging, and development projects within the Bay, along the Bay shoreline, and within salt ponds and certain  

[12:5:66,693] managed wetlands adjacent to the Bay. The Commission also implements the Suisun Marsh Preservation Act of 1977. By  

[12:6:66,704] statute, the Commission develops and implements the federal Coastal Zone Management Act's program for the Bay and  

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:81,69] 3825   San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and Mountains Conservancy  

[2:0:66,91] The San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and Mountains Conservancy was established in 1999 to acquire and manage  

[2:1:66,101] public lands. Through these acquisitions, the Conservancy provides for additional open space, low-impact recreational uses,  

[2:2:66,111] educational uses, water conservation and watershed improvement, and wildlife and habitat restoration and protection. These  

[2:3:66,121] acquisitions also help to preserve the San Gabriel Mountains and the San Gabriel and Lower Los Angeles Rivers and their  

[2:4:66,131] tributaries, consistent with existing and adopted river and flood control projects for the protection of life and property.

[11:0:195,577] 3830   San Joaquin River Conservancy  

[12:0:66,599] The mission of the San Joaquin River Conservancy is to acquire, preserve, manage, and promote access to lands within the  

[12:1:66,609] flood plain on both sides of the San Joaquin River from Friant Dam to Highway 99.  

[12:2:66,627] Specific activities are to:  

[13:0:65,644] • Implement the San Joaquin River Parkway Master Plan, a 22-mile regional greenspace and wildlife corridor along both sides  

[13:1:76,655] of the river extending from Friant Dam to Highway 99, with an interconnected trail system and recreational and educational  

[13:2:76,665] features.  

[14:0:65,681] • Operate and manage lands for public access, recreation, and enjoyment consistent with the protection of natural and cultural  

[14:1:76,691] resources.  

[15:0:65,707] • Protect, enhance, and restore riparian and riverine habitat and ecological diversity.  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES

[5:0:210,221] 3835   Baldwin Hills Conservancy  

[5:1:66,243] The Baldwin Hills Conservancy was established in 2000 to acquire open space and manage public lands within the Baldwin  

[5:2:66,253] Hills area and to provide recreation, restoration, and protection of wildlife habitat for the public's enjoyment and educational  

[5:3:66,263] experience.  

[5:4:66,283] Specific agency responsibilities are to:  

[5:5:65,301] • Approve the Baldwin Hills Park Master Plan.  

[6:0:65,317] • Undertake site improvement projects, regulate public access, construct new facilities as needed for outdoor recreation, and  

[6:1:76,328] provide for the appreciation and protection of natural resources.  

[6:2:65,344] • Prioritize and implement acquisition of additional recreational and open space land within the Baldwin Hills area.  

[6:3:65,360] • Provide recreational, open space, wildlife habitat restoration and protection of lands for educational use within the area.  

[6:4:65,376] • Conduct the necessary planning activities for the area.  

[6:5:65,392] • Approve conservancy funded projects that advance the policies and priorities set forth by the enabling statute.  

[6:6:65,409] • Develop and coordinate a program of resource stewardship for optimum recreational and natural resource value based on  

[6:7:76,419] the needs of the surrounding community.  

[7:0:65,435] • Provide for the public's enjoyment, and enhance recreational and educational experiences on public lands in a manner  

[7:1:76,445] consistent with the protection of lands and resources in the area.

[7:0:203,282] 3840   Delta Protection Commission  

[8:0:66,303] The mission of the Delta Protection Commission is to protect, maintain, enhance, and enrich the overall quality of the Delta  

[8:1:66,313] environment and economy, with a focus on agriculture, recreation, and natural resources,   while remaining   mindful of the  

[8:2:66,323] importance of the Delta to all Californians. The Commission   supports orderly, balanced conservation and development of Delta  

[8:3:66,334] land resources and provides a forum for Delta residents to engage in decisions regarding actions to recognize and enhance the  

[8:4:66,344] unique cultural, recreational, and agricultural resources of the Delta. The Commission identifies and makes recommendations  

[8:5:66,354] on methods of preserving the Delta as an evolving place and promotes Delta legacy communities, regional economic  

[8:6:66,364] sustainability, and emergency response preparedness.

[5:0:202,180] 3845   San Diego River Conservancy  

[6:0:66,202] The mission of the San Diego River Conservancy is to implement the San Diego River Conservancy Act through programs  

[6:1:66,212] emphasizing Land Conservation, Recreation and Education, Natural and Cultural Resources, Water Quality and Natural Flood  

[6:2:66,222] Conveyance, Organizational Capacity, and the San Diego Rivers Watershed Consortium. This Conservancy’s mission will be  

[6:3:66,232] accomplished in part by building with our partners the San Diego River Trail stretching over 52 miles from the headwaters in  

[6:4:66,243] Julian to the Pacific Ocean. The Conservancy has established advisory panels to assist in restoring and enhancing three  

[6:5:66,253] nearby watersheds: the Otay River, Sweetwater River, and Tijuana River. Working with stakeholders within these watersheds  

[6:6:66,263] fosters collaboration among governments, public agencies and nonprofit organizations, and leverages grant funding to achieve  

[6:7:66,273] an array of conservation objectives for the region with an emphasis on native habitat restoration and mitigating the impacts of  

[6:8:66,283] climate change, seawater intrusion in local watersheds, sea level rise, and the risk of wildfire and flooding.

[11:0:169,553] 3850   Coachella Valley Mountains Conservancy  

[12:0:66,575] The mission of the Coachella Valley Mountains Conservancy is to acquire and hold as open space the mountainous lands  

[12:1:66,585] surrounding the Coachella Valley and the natural lands within the Coachella Valley. In addition, the Conservancy works to  

[12:2:66,595] protect wildlife and enhance recreational and educational experiences on those lands, to conserve local water resources and to  

[12:3:66,605] promote sustainability and climate change adaptability within the desert and surrounding mountain areas.

[6:0:208,221] 3855   Sierra Nevada Conservancy  

[6:1:66,243] The Sierra Nevada Conservancy's mission is to initiate, encourage, and support efforts that improve the environmental,  

[6:2:66,253] economic, and social well-being of the Sierra Nevada Region, its communities and the people of California. The Conservancy  

[6:3:66,263] achieves its mission through the awarding of grants and loans, development of projects and programs, providing strategic  

[6:4:66,273] direction and resources to the Region, and assisting in collaborative efforts with a broad array of governmental and non-

[6:5:66,283] governmental partners.

[11:1:66,620] 3220 - SIERRA NEVADA CONSERVANCY PROGRAM  

[12:0:66,641] Established pursuant to Chapter 726, Statutes of 2004, the Conservancy is authorized to:  

[12:1:65,658] • Provide increased opportunities for tourism and recreation.  

[12:2:65,675] • Protect, conserve, and restore the region's physical, cultural, archaeological, historical, and living resources.  

[12:3:65,691] • Aid in the preservation of working landscapes.  

[12:4:65,707] • Reduce the risk of natural disasters, such as wildfires.

[7:0:196,334] 3860   Department of Water Resources  

[8:0:66,356] The Department of Water Resources protects, conserves, develops, and manages California's water supply. The Department  

[8:1:66,366] evaluates existing water resources, forecasts future water needs, and explores potential solutions to meet the state’s growing  

[8:2:66,376] needs for personal use, irrigation, industry, recreation, power generation, and fish and wildlife. The Department also works to  

[8:3:66,386] prevent and minimize flood damage, oversee the safety of dams, and educate the public about the importance of water and its  

[8:4:66,396] efficient use.  

[9:0:66,417] Because the Department's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay program to  

[9:1:66,427] support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[4:0:66,126] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[5:0:65,145] • Energy Package—The Budget includes $1.5 billion General Fund in 2021-22, and $700 million General Fund in 2022-23, as  

[5:1:76,156] part of a $8.1 billion package of investments to support statewide energy reliability. This includes funding to support a  

[5:2:76,166] strategic reliability reserve. Of the total $8.1 billion, $3.8 billion will be allocated at a later time.  

[6:0:65,182] • Drought Resilience and Response Package—The Budget includes $571 million California Emergency Relief Fund in  

[6:1:76,192] 2021-22, and $50 million California Emergency Relief Fund and $2.1 million General Fund in 2022-23, as part of a $2.7  

[6:2:76,202] billion package of investments to support the state’s response to continued drought conditions. This includes funding to  

[6:3:76,213] support small and urban water suppliers, conservation efforts, and a turf replacement program, among other investments. Of  

[6:4:76,223] the total $2.7 billion, $1.5 billion will be allocated at a later time.  

[7:0:65,239] • Water Resilience and Drought Package—The Budget includes $376.6 million General Fund in 2022-23, and $200 million  

[7:1:76,249] General Fund in 2023-24, as part of a $5.2 billion package to support water resilience and drought response efforts. This  

[7:2:76,259] includes funding to support flood control projects, Sustainable Groundwater Management Act implementation, the Salton  

[7:3:76,270] Sea, and water conveyance projects.  

[8:0:65,286] • Climate Resilience Package—The Budget includes $125 million General Fund in 2022-23, and $75 million General Fund in  

[8:1:76,296] 2023-24, as part of a $3.7 billion package of investments that addresses the state’s climate risks. This includes funding to  

[8:2:76,306] support habitat restoration projects that will enhance ecosystem health while improving water infrastructure.  

[9:0:65,322] • California Electric Grid Emergency Generator Support—The Budget includes $15.5 million General Fund in 2022-23, and  

[9:1:76,333] $14.8 million General Fund in 2023-24, to support the operational needs of energy generators purchased to support  

[9:2:76,343] California’s electric grid in response to the July 2021 Energy Emergency Proclamation.

[7:15:75,492] Totals, Budget Adjustments  $2,048,680 $574,108 434.2 $1,170,777 $110,607 369.9  

[8:0:66,524] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[9:0:66,544] 3230 - CONTINUING FORMULATION OF THE CALIFORNIA WATER PLAN  

[10:0:66,561] The California Water Plan is the state's strategic plan for the efficient use, management and development of the state's water  

[10:1:66,572] resources. The Plan is updated every five years and provides a framework for water managers, legislators, and the public to  

[10:2:66,582] consider options and make decisions regarding California's water future. The Plan evaluates current and future water  

[10:3:66,592] conditions, challenges and opportunities. It presents basic data and information on California's water resources including water  

[10:4:66,602] supply evaluations and assessments of existing and future agricultural, urban, and environmental water uses. The Plan  

[10:5:66,612] identifies and evaluates multiple future scenarios and over 30 resource management strategies such as conservation,  

[10:6:66,623] recycling, desalination, transfers, storage, conveyance, water quality, watershed management, ecosystem restoration,  

[10:7:66,633] groundwater, and urban land use management to help meet future demands in light of uncertainties and unexpected  

[10:8:66,643] catastrophic events. This program also identifies ways for the state to: (1) help local agencies and governments prepare  

[10:9:66,653] integrated regional water management plans on a watershed basis and diversify their regional water portfolios to ensure  

[10:10:66,663] sustainable water uses, reliable water supplies, better water quality, environmental stewardship, efficient urban development,  

[10:11:66,674] protection of agriculture, and supporting a strong economy, (2) help cities, counties and local agencies prepare a Water  

[10:12:66,684] Element for their General Plans, Urban Water Management Plans and Agricultural Water Management Plans, and (3) help local  

[10:13:66,694] agencies and tribal governments improve water and land use planning coordination.  

[11:0:66,712] 3240 - IMPLEMENTATION OF THE STATE WATER RESOURCES DEVELOPMENT SYSTEM  

[12:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 236 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2022-23 STATE BUDGET — RES   234 

[1:0:454,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:161,32] 3860   Department of Water Resources - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] The State Water Project is a water storage and delivery system that consists of 34 storage facilities (reservoirs and lakes), 21  

[3:1:66,79] pumping plants, 4 pumping-generating plants, 5 hydroelectric power plants, and over 700 miles of conveyance (canals,  

[3:2:66,89] pipelines, and tunnels). The Project provides water to over 25 million Californians and 750,000 acres of irrigated farmland.  

[4:0:66,107] The Department plans, designs, constructs, operates, maintains, and manages State Water Project facilities that provide water  

[4:1:66,117] to facilities located from Plumas County to Riverside County.  

[5:0:66,135] The Delta Conveyance Program is charged with improving the Delta ecosystem and ensuring water supply reliability in a safe,  

[5:1:66,145] timely, and cost-effective manner. This includes implementation of the Delta Conveyance, project specific environmental  

[5:2:66,155] commitments, and compliance with the Environmental Impact Report and Environmental Impact Statement and other state and  

[5:3:66,165] federal regulations and permits.  

[6:0:66,183] 3245 - PUBLIC SAFETY AND PREVENTION OF DAMAGE  

[7:0:66,201] This program supports the California Water Action Plan by protecting life and property from damage by floods, ensures proper  

[7:1:66,211] construction and maintenance of jurisdictional dams and levees, and provides loans for levee construction. Activities include  

[7:2:66,221] assessing the state and regional investment needs to reduce risk, preventive floodplain management to discourage unwise use  

[7:3:66,231] of areas subject to flooding, protection of floodplains, issuance of flood warnings, operation of flood control facilities,  

[7:4:66,241] coordination and supervision of flood fight activities, and annual levee and flood channel maintenance and inspection in  

[7:5:66,252] cooperation with other local, state, and federal partner agencies. This program also buys land, easements, and rights-of-way  

[7:6:66,262] for federal flood control projects and supervises the design and construction of new dams and periodic inspection and the  

[7:7:66,272] reevaluation of all existing jurisdictional dams for proper operation and maintenance. Fiscal oversight and coordination activities  

[7:8:66,282] associated with the Disaster Preparedness and Flood Prevention Bond Act of 2006 are administered under this program. The  

[7:9:66,292] program also reviews federal dam projects in coordination with federal and other state agencies with regard to dam safety.  

[8:0:66,310] 3250 - CENTRAL VALLEY FLOOD PROTECTION BOARD  

[9:0:66,331] The Central Valley Flood Protection Board has regulatory authority over the State Plan of Flood Control (SPFC) facilities,  

[9:1:66,341] designated floodways, and regulated streams in the Central Valley. The Board regulates encroachments on the system by  

[9:2:66,351] issuing permits and initiating enforcement action when necessary to maintain the integrity of the levees and floodways that  

[9:3:66,361] protect the valley's people and property. The Board manages the state's portfolio of real property held by the Sacramento-San  

[9:4:66,371] Joaquin Drainage District. The Board serves as the non-federal sponsor to the United States Army Corps of Engineers on large  

[9:5:66,382] joint state-federal levee improvement projects and assists the more than   100 local maintaining agencies that operate and  

[9:6:66,392] maintain the SPFC. The Board conducts regular public meetings, workshops and tours, providing a public forum for  

[9:7:66,402] stakeholders.  

[10:0:66,420] 3255 - SERVICES  

[11:0:66,437] This program provides technical support within the Department and expertise in the fields of water resources planning,  

[11:1:66,448] development and management; watermaster services; scientific analyses performed by DWR's chemical laboratory;  

[11:2:66,458] information technology; and mapping, surveying and engineering services for other agencies.  

[12:0:66,475] 3260 - CALIFORNIA ENERGY BOND OFFICE  

[13:0:66,493] California Energy Bond Office is responsible for managing the Electrical Power Fund and the Department of Water Resources  

[13:1:66,503] (DWR) Charge Fund. In response to the 2001 California energy crisis, for a limited period of time, the Electrical Power Charge  

[13:2:66,514] Fund purchased electric power on behalf of the state's investor-owned utilities and issued $11.3 billion in bonds. The final  

[13:3:66,524] amount of the outstanding bonds were defeased in September 2020. Bond proceeds were used to repay amounts borrowed for  

[13:4:66,534] power purchases during the 2001 energy crisis.   The DWR Charge Fund was created in 2019 by Chapter 79, Statutes of 2019  

[13:5:66,544] (AB 1054) to collect remittances from charges on investor-owned utilities ratepayers pursuant to Water Code Section 80550.

[4:0:66,131] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[5:0:66,150] The Department of Water Resources has two major infrastructure programs: the State Water Project and the flood control  

[5:1:66,161] system in the Central Valley. The State Water Project is a water storage and delivery system that consists of 34 storage  

[5:2:66,171] facilities, reservoirs, and lakes, 21 pumping plants, 4 pumping-generating plants, 5 hydroelectric power plants, and over 700  

[5:3:66,181] miles of canals, pipelines, and tunnels. The Project provides water to 25 million Californians and 750,000 acres of irrigated  

[5:4:66,191] farmland. The Project also provides recreational opportunities to approximately 6 million visitors annually at 15 separate  

[5:5:66,201] reservoir-based facilities as well as public fishing access to much of the Aqueduct. Existing flood control infrastructure in the  

[5:6:66,212] Central Valley consists of 1,595 miles of levees, 348,000 acres of channels and floodways, over 800,000 linear feet of bank  

[5:7:66,222] protection, over 60 mitigation and environmental restoration sites, and 55 various flood control structures including dams, weirs,  

[5:8:66,232] pumping plants, diversion structures, gate structures, and drop structures. In general, these facilities provide more flood  

[5:9:66,242] protection in urbanized areas and less in agricultural areas.

[7:0:159,294] 3875   Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta Conservancy  

[8:0:66,316] The mission of the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta Conservancy is to act as the primary state agency to implement ecosystem  

[8:1:66,326] restoration in the Delta and   support efforts that advance both environmental protection and the economic well-being of Delta  

[8:2:66,336] residents in a complementary manner. The Conservancy's activities include protecting and enhancing habitat and habitat  

[8:3:66,346] restoration; protecting and preserving Delta agriculture and working landscapes; providing increased opportunities for tourism  

[8:4:66,356] and recreation; promoting Delta legacy communities and economic vitality in the Delta; increasing the resilience of the Delta to  

[8:5:66,367] the effects of climate change and natural disasters such as floods and earthquakes; protecting and improving water quality;  

[8:6:66,377] assisting the Delta regional economy;   conducting activities to protect, conserve, and restore the region's physical, agricultural,  

[8:7:66,387] cultural, historical, and living resources; assisting local entities in the implementation of their habitat conservation plans and  

[8:8:66,397] natural community conservation plans; facilitating protection and safe harbor agreements under the federal Endangered  

[8:9:66,407] Species Act of 1973 and the California Endangered Species Act for adjacent land owners and local public agencies; and  

[8:10:66,418] promoting environmental education.

[5:0:211,140] 3885   Delta Stewardship Council  

[6:0:66,162] Established in 2009 by the Delta Reform Act, the mission of the Delta Stewardship Council, through a seven-member board, is  

[6:1:66,172] to further the state's coequal goals of providing a more reliable water supply for California and protecting, restoring, and  

[6:2:66,182] enhancing the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta ecosystem. The goals are to be achieved in a manner that protects and  

[6:3:66,192] enhances the unique cultural, recreational, natural resource, and agricultural values of the Delta. In 2013, the Council adopted  

[6:4:66,203] a legally enforceable Delta Plan to further the coequal goals and guide state and local agency activities related to the Delta.  

[6:5:66,213] Under state law, agencies are required to coordinate their actions pursuant to the Delta Plan with the Council and the other  

[6:6:66,223] relevant agencies.   The Council is informed by scientific input from the Delta Science Program and the Delta Independent  

[6:7:66,233] Science Board. The mission of the Delta Science Program is to provide the best possible unbiased scientific information to  

[6:8:66,243] inform water and environmental decisionmaking in the Delta.
